Mean and\\n755:     standard deviation are then stored to be used on later data using\\n756:     :meth:`transform`.\\n757: \\n758:     Standardization of a dataset is a common requirement for many\\n759:     machine learning estimators: they might behave badly if the\\n760:     individual features do not more or less look like standard normally\\n761:     distributed data (e.g. Gaussian with 0 mean and unit variance).\\n762: \\n763:     For instance many elements used in the objective function of\\n764:     a learning algorithm (such as the RBF kernel of Support Vector\\n765:     Machines or the L1 and L2 regularizers of linear models) assume that\\n766:     all features are centered around 0 and have variance in the same\\n767:     order. If a feature has a variance that is orders of magnitude larger\\n768:     than others, it might dominate the objective function and make the\\n769:     estimator unable to learn from other features correctly as expected.\\n770: \\n771:     `StandardScaler` is sensitive to outliers, and the features may scale\\n772:     differently from each other in the presence of outliers. For an example\\n773:     visualization, refer to :ref:`Compare StandardScaler with other scalers\\n774:     <plot_all_scaling_standard_scaler_section>`.\\n775: \\n776:     This scaler can also be applied to sparse CSR or CSC matrices by passing\\n777:     `with_mean=False` to avoid breaking the sparsity structure of the data.\\n778: \\n779:     Read more in the :ref:`User Guide <preprocessing_scaler>`.\\n780: \\n781:     Parameters\\n782:     ----------\\n783:     copy : bool, default=True\\n784:         If False, try to avoid a copy and do inplace scaling instead.\\n785:         This is not guaranteed to always work inplace; e.g. if the data is\\n786:         not a NumPy array or scipy.sparse CSR matrix, a copy may still be\\n787:         returned.\\n788: \\n789:     with_mean : bool, default=True\\n790:         If True, center the data before scaling.\\n791:         This does not work (and will raise an exception) when attempted on\\n792:         sparse matrices, because centering them entails building a dense\\n793:         matrix which in common use cases is likely to be too large to fit in\\n794:         memory.\\n795: \\n796:     with_std : bool, default=True\\n797:         If True, scale the data to unit variance (or equivalently,\\n798:         unit standard deviation).\\n799: \\n800:     Attributes\\n801:     ----------\\n802:     scale_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\\n803:         Per feature relative scaling of the data to achieve zero mean and unit\\n804:         variance. Generally this is calculated using `np.sqrt(var_)`. If a\\n805:         variance is zero, we can't achieve unit variance, and the data is left\\n806:         as-is, giving a scaling factor of 1. `scale_` is equal to `None`\\n807:         when `with_std=False`.\\n808: \\n809:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n810:            *scale_*\\n811: \\n812:     mean_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\\n813:         The mean value for each feature in the training set.\\n814:         Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and ``with_std=False``.\\n815: \\n816:     var_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\\n817:         The variance for each feature in the training set. Used to compute\\n818:         `scale_`. Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and\\n819:         ``with_std=False``.\\n820: \\n821:     n_features_in_ : int\\n822:         Number of features seen during :term:`fit`.\\n823: \\n824:         .. versionadded:: 0.24\\n825: \\n826:     feature_names_in_ : ndarray of shape (`n_features_in_`,)\\n827:         Names of features seen during :term:`fit`. Defined only when `X`\\n828:         has feature names that are all strings.\\n829: \\n830:         .. versionadded:: 1.0\\n831: \\n832:     n_samples_seen_ : int or ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n833:         The number of samples processed by the estimator for each feature.\\n834:         If there are no missing samples, the ``n_samples_seen`` will be an\\n835:         integer, otherwise it will be an array of dtype int. If\\n836:         `sample_weights` are used it will be a float (if no missing data)\\n837:         or an array of dtype float that sums the weights seen so far.\\n838:         Will be reset on new calls to fit, but increments across\\n839:         ``partial_fit`` calls.\\n840: \\n841:     See Also\\n842:     --------\\n843:     scale : Equivalent function without the estimator API.\\n844: \\n845:     :class:`~sklearn.decomposition.PCA` : Further removes the linear\\n846:         correlation across features with 'whiten=True'.\\n847: \\n848:     Notes\\n849:     -----\\n850:     NaNs are treated as missing values: disregarded in fit, and maintained in\\n851:     transform.\\n852: \\n853:     We use a biased estimator for the standard deviation, equivalent to\\n854:     `numpy.std(x, ddof=0)`. Note that the choice of `ddof` is unlikely to\\n855:     affect model performance.\\n856: \\n857:     Examples\\n858:     --------\\n859:     >>> from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler\\n860:     >>> data = [[0, 0], [0, 0], [1, 1], [1, 1]]\\n861:     >>> scaler = StandardScaler()\\n862:     >>> print(scaler.fit(data))\\n863:     StandardScaler()\\n864:     >>> print(scaler.mean_)\\n865:     [0.5 0.5]\\n866:     >>> print(scaler.transform(data))\\n867:     [[-1. -1.]\\n868:      [-1. -1.]\\n869:      [ 1.  1.]\\n870:      [ 1.  1.]]\\n871:     >>> print(scaler.transform([[2, 2]]))\\n872:     [[3. 3.]]\\n873:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n874: \\n875:     _parameter_constraints: dict = {\\n876:         \\\"copy\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n877:         \\\"with_mean\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n878:         \\\"with_std\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n879:     }\\n880: \\n881:     def __init__(self, *, copy=True, with_mean=True, with_std=True):\\n882:         self.with_mean = with_mean\\n883:         self.with_std = with_std\\n884:         self.copy = copy\\n885: \\n886:     def _reset(self):\\n887:         \\\"\\\"\\\"Reset internal data-dependent state of the scaler, if necessary.\\n888: \\n889:         __init__ parameters are not touched.\\n890:         \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n891:         # Mean and\\n755:     standard deviation are then stored to be used on later data using\\n756:     :meth:`transform`.\\n757: \\n758:     Standardization of a dataset is a common requirement for many\\n759:     machine learning estimators: they might behave badly if the\\n760:     individual features do not more or less look like standard normally\\n761:     distributed data (e.g. Gaussian with 0 mean and unit variance).\\n762: \\n763:     For instance many elements used in the objective function of\\n764:     a learning algorithm (such as the RBF kernel of Support Vector\\n765:     Machines or the L1 and L2 regularizers of linear models) assume that\\n766:     all features are centered around 0 and have variance in the same\\n767:     order. If a feature has a variance that is orders of magnitude larger\\n768:     than others, it might dominate the objective function and make the\\n769:     estimator unable to learn from other features correctly as expected.\\n770: \\n771:     `StandardScaler` is sensitive to outliers, and the features may scale\\n772:     differently from each other in the presence of outliers. For an example\\n773:     visualization, refer to :ref:`Compare StandardScaler with other scalers\\n774:     <plot_all_scaling_standard_scaler_section>`.\\n775: \\n776:     This scaler can also be applied to sparse CSR or CSC matrices by passing\\n777:     `with_mean=False` to avoid breaking the sparsity structure of the data.\\n778: \\n779:     Read more in the :ref:`User Guide <preprocessing_scaler>`.\\n780: \\n781:     Parameters\\n782:     ----------\\n783:     copy : bool, default=True\\n784:         If False, try to avoid a copy and do inplace scaling instead.\\n785:         This is not guaranteed to always work inplace; e.g. if the data is\\n786:         not a NumPy array or scipy.sparse CSR matrix, a copy may still be\\n787:         returned.\\n788: \\n789:     with_mean : bool, default=True\\n790:         If True, center the data before scaling.\\n791:         This does not work (and will raise an exception) when attempted on\\n792:         sparse matrices, because centering them entails building a dense\\n793:         matrix which in common use cases is likely to be too large to fit in\\n794:         memory.\\n795: \\n796:     with_std : bool, default=True\\n797:         If True, scale the data to unit variance (or equivalently,\\n798:         unit standard deviation).\\n799: \\n800:     Attributes\\n801:     ----------\\n802:     scale_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\\n803:         Per feature relative scaling of the data to achieve zero mean and unit\\n804:         variance. Generally this is calculated using `np.sqrt(var_)`. If a\\n805:         variance is zero, we can't achieve unit variance, and the data is left\\n806:         as-is, giving a scaling factor of 1. `scale_` is equal to `None`\\n807:         when `with_std=False`.\\n808: \\n809:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n810:            *scale_*\\n811: \\n812:     mean_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\\n813:         The mean value for each feature in the training set.\\n814:         Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and ``with_std=False``.\\n815: \\n816:     var_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\\n817:         The variance for each feature in the training set. Used to compute\\n818:         `scale_`. Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and\\n819:         ``with_std=False``.\\n820: \\n821:     n_features_in_ : int\\n822:         Number of features seen during :term:`fit`.\\n823: \\n824:         .. versionadded:: 0.24\\n825: \\n826:     feature_names_in_ : ndarray of shape (`n_features_in_`,)\\n827:         Names of features seen during :term:`fit`. Defined only when `X`\\n828:         has feature names that are all strings.\\n829: \\n830:         .. versionadded:: 1.0\\n831: \\n832:     n_samples_seen_ : int or ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n833:         The number of samples processed by the estimator for each feature.\\n834:         If there are no missing samples, the ``n_samples_seen`` will be an\\n835:         integer, otherwise it will be an array of dtype int. If\\n836:         `sample_weights` are used it will be a float (if no missing data)\\n837:         or an array of dtype float that sums the weights seen so far.\\n838:         Will be reset on new calls to fit, but increments across\\n839:         ``partial_fit`` calls.\\n840: \\n841:     See Also\\n842:     --------\\n843:     scale : Equivalent function without the estimator API.\\n844: \\n845:     :class:`~sklearn.decomposition.PCA` : Further removes the linear\\n846:         correlation across features with 'whiten=True'.\\n847: \\n848:     Notes\\n849:     -----\\n850:     NaNs are treated as missing values: disregarded in fit, and maintained in\\n851:     transform.\\n852: \\n853:     We use a biased estimator for the standard deviation, equivalent to\\n854:     `numpy.std(x, ddof=0)`. Note that the choice of `ddof` is unlikely to\\n855:     affect model performance.\\n856: \\n857:     Examples\\n858:     --------\\n859:     >>> from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler\\n860:     >>> data = [[0, 0], [0, 0], [1, 1], [1, 1]]\\n861:     >>> scaler = StandardScaler()\\n862:     >>> print(scaler.fit(data))\\n863:     StandardScaler()\\n864:     >>> print(scaler.mean_)\\n865:     [0.5 0.5]\\n866:     >>> print(scaler.transform(data))\\n867:     [[-1. -1.]\\n868:      [-1. -1.]\\n869:      [ 1.  1.]\\n870:      [ 1.  1.]]\\n871:     >>> print(scaler.transform([[2, 2]]))\\n872:     [[3. 3.]]\\n873:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n874: \\n875:     _parameter_constraints: dict = {\\n876:         \\\"copy\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n877:         \\\"with_mean\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n878:         \\\"with_std\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n879:     }\\n880: \\n881:     def __init__(self, *, copy=True, with_mean=True, with_std=True):\\n882:         self.with_mean = with_mean\\n883:         self.with_std = with_std\\n884:         self.copy = copy\\n885: \\n886:     def _reset(self):\\n887:         \\\"\\\"\\\"Reset internal data-dependent state of the scaler, if necessary.\\n888: \\n889:         __init__ parameters are not touched.\\n890:         \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n891:         # Mean and\n755:     standard deviation are then stored to be used on later data using\n756:     :meth:`transform`.\n757: \n758:     Standardization of a dataset is a common requirement for many\n759:     machine learning estimators: they might behave badly if the\n760:     individual features do not more or less look like standard normally\n761:     distributed data (e.g. Gaussian with 0 mean and unit variance).\n762: \n763:     For instance many elements used in the objective function of\n764:     a learning algorithm (such as the RBF kernel of Support Vector\n765:     Machines or the L1 and L2 regularizers of linear models) assume that\n766:     all features are centered around 0 and have variance in the same\n767:     order. If a feature has a variance that is orders of magnitude larger\n768:     than others, it might dominate the objective function and make the\n769:     estimator unable to learn from other features correctly as expected.\n770: \n771:     `StandardScaler` is sensitive to outliers, and the features may scale\n772:     differently from each other in the presence of outliers. For an example\n773:     visualization, refer to :ref:`Compare StandardScaler with other scalers\n774:     <plot_all_scaling_standard_scaler_section>`.\n775: \n776:     This scaler can also be applied to sparse CSR or CSC matrices by passing\n777:     `with_mean=False` to avoid breaking the sparsity structure of the data.\n778: \n779:     Read more in the :ref:`User Guide <preprocessing_scaler>`.\n780: \n781:     Parameters\n782:     ----------\n783:     copy : bool, default=True\n784:         If False, try to avoid a copy and do inplace scaling instead.\n785:         This is not guaranteed to always work inplace; e.g. if the data is\n786:         not a NumPy array or scipy.sparse CSR matrix, a copy may still be\n787:         returned.\n788: \n789:     with_mean : bool, default=True\n790:         If True, center the data before scaling.\n791:         This does not work (and will raise an exception) when attempted on\n792:         sparse matrices, because centering them entails building a dense\n793:         matrix which in common use cases is likely to be too large to fit in\n794:         memory.\n795: \n796:     with_std : bool, default=True\n797:         If True, scale the data to unit variance (or equivalently,\n798:         unit standard deviation).\n799: \n800:     Attributes\n801:     ----------\n802:     scale_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\n803:         Per feature relative scaling of the data to achieve zero mean and unit\n804:         variance. Generally this is calculated using `np.sqrt(var_)`. If a\n805:         variance is zero, we can't achieve unit variance, and the data is left\n806:         as-is, giving a scaling factor of 1. `scale_` is equal to `None`\n807:         when `with_std=False`.\n808: \n809:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\n810:            *scale_*\n811: \n812:     mean_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\n813:         The mean value for each feature in the training set.\n814:         Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and ``with_std=False``.\n815: \n816:     var_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\n817:         The variance for each feature in the training set. Used to compute\n818:         `scale_`. Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and\n819:         ``with_std=False``.\n820: \n821:     n_features_in_ : int\n822:         Number of features seen during :term:`fit`.\n823: \n824:         .. versionadded:: 0.24\n825: \n826:     feature_names_in_ : ndarray of shape (`n_features_in_`,)\n827:         Names of features seen during :term:`fit`. Defined only when `X`\n828:         has feature names that are all strings.\n829: \n830:         .. versionadded:: 1.0\n831: \n832:     n_samples_seen_ : int or ndarray of shape (n_features,)\n833:         The number of samples processed by the estimator for each feature.\n834:         If there are no missing samples, the ``n_samples_seen`` will be an\n835:         integer, otherwise it will be an array of dtype int. If\n836:         `sample_weights` are used it will be a float (if no missing data)\n837:         or an array of dtype float that sums the weights seen so far.\n838:         Will be reset on new calls to fit, but increments across\n839:         ``partial_fit`` calls.\n840: \n841:     See Also\n842:     --------\n843:     scale : Equivalent function without the estimator API.\n844: \n845:     :class:`~sklearn.decomposition.PCA` : Further removes the linear\n846:         correlation across features with 'whiten=True'.\n847: \n848:     Notes\n849:     -----\n850:     NaNs are treated as missing values: disregarded in fit, and maintained in\n851:     transform.\n852: \n853:     We use a biased estimator for the standard deviation, equivalent to\n854:     `numpy.std(x, ddof=0)`. LeVeque. \"Algorithms\n936:         for computing the sample variance: Analysis and recommendations.\"\n937:         The American Statistician 37.3 (1983): 242-247:\n938: \n939:         Parameters\n940:         ----------\n941:         X : {array-like, sparse matrix} of shape (n_samples, n_features)\n942:             The data used to compute the mean and standard deviation\n943:             used for later scaling along the features axis.\n944: \n945:         y : None\n946:             Ignored.\n947: \n948:         sample_weight : array-like of shape (n_samples,), default=None\n949:             Individual weights for each sample.\n950: \n951:             .. versionadded:: 0.24\n952:                parameter *sample_weight* support to StandardScaler.\n953: \n954:         Returns\n955:         -------\n956:         self : object\n957:             Fitted scaler.\n958:         \"\"\"\n959:         xp, _, X_device = get_namespace_and_device(X)\n960:         first_call = not hasattr(self, \"n_samples_seen_\")\n961:         X = validate_data(\n962:             self,\n963:             X,\n964:             accept_sparse=(\"csr\", \"csc\"),\n965:             dtype=supported_float_dtypes(xp, X_device),\n966:             ensure_all_finite=\"allow-nan\",\n967:             reset=first_call,\n968:         )\n969:         n_features = X.shape[1]\n970: \n971: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/scikit-learn--cb7e82dd@72904752e88f414cbc543dac5945b6c8dd7b34fa/-/blob/sklearn/preprocessing/_data.py%3FL899-970"}}}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01FWTvsCuhEgt75JTBgSW2Vh","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"303: class MinMaxScaler(OneToOneFeatureMixin, TransformerMixin, BaseEstimator):\\n304:     \\\"\\\"\\\"Transform features by scaling each feature to a given range.\\n305: \\n306:     This estimator scales and translates each feature individually such\\n307:     that it is in the given range on the training set, e.g. between\\n308:     zero and one.\\n309: \\n310:     The transformation is given by::\\n311: \\n312:         X_std = (X - X.min(axis=0)) / (X.max(axis=0) - X.min(axis=0))\\n313:         X_scaled = X_std * (max - min) + min\\n314: \\n315:     where min, max = feature_range.\\n316: \\n317:     This transformation is often used as an alternative to zero mean,\\n318:     unit variance scaling.\\n319: \\n320:     `MinMaxScaler` doesn't reduce the effect of outliers, but it linearly\\n321:     scales them down into a fixed range, where the largest occurring data point\\n322:     corresponds to the maximum value and the smallest one corresponds to the\\n323:     minimum value. For an example visualization, refer to :ref:`Compare\\n324:     MinMaxScaler with other scalers <plot_all_scaling_minmax_scaler_section>`.\\n325: \\n326:     Read more in the :ref:`User Guide <preprocessing_scaler>`.\\n327: \\n328:     Parameters\\n329:     ----------\\n330:     feature_range : tuple (min, max), default=(0, 1)\\n331:         Desired range of transformed data.\\n332: \\n333:     copy : bool, default=True\\n334:         Set to False to perform inplace row normalization and avoid a\\n335:         copy (if the input is already a numpy array).\\n336: \\n337:     clip : bool, default=False\\n338:         Set to True to clip transformed values of held-out data to\\n339:         provided `feature_range`.\\n340:         Since this parameter will clip values, `inverse_transform` may not\\n341:         be able to restore the original data.\\n342: \\n343:         .. note::\\n344:             Setting `clip=True` does not prevent feature drift (a distribution\\n345:             shift between training and test data). The transformed values are clipped\\n346:             to the `feature_range`, which helps avoid unintended behavior in models\\n347:             sensitive to out-of-range inputs (e.g. linear models). Equivalent to\\n360:         ``(max - min) / (X.max(axis=0) - X.min(axis=0))``\\n361: \\n362:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n363:            *scale_* attribute.\\n364: \\n365:     data_min_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n366:         Per feature minimum seen in the data\\n367: \\n368:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n369:            *data_min_*\\n370: \\n371:     data_max_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n372:         Per feature maximum seen in the data\\n373: \\n374:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n375:            *data_max_*\\n376: \\n377:     data_range_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n378:         Per feature range ``(data_max_ - data_min_)`` seen in the data\\n379: \\n380:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n381:            *data_range_*\\n382: \\n383:     n_features_in_ : int\\n384:         Number of features seen during :term:`fit`.\\n385: \\n386:         .. versionadded:: 0.24\\n387: \\n388:     n_samples_seen_ : int\\n389:         The number of samples processed by the estimator.\\n390:         It will be reset on new calls to fit, but increments across\\n391:         ``partial_fit`` calls.\\n392: \\n393:     feature_names_in_ : ndarray of shape (`n_features_in_`,)\\n394:         Names of features seen during :term:`fit`. Defined only when `X`\\n395:         has feature names that are all strings.\\n396: \\n397:         .. versionadded:: 1.0\\n398: \\n399:     See Also\\n400:     --------\\n401:     minmax_scale : Equivalent function without the estimator API.\\n402: \\n403:     Notes\\n404:     -----\\n405:     NaNs are treated as missing values: disregarded in fit, and maintained in\\n406:     transform.\\n407: \\n408:     Examples\\n409:     --------\\n410:     >>> from sklearn.preprocessing import MinMaxScaler\\n411:     >>> data = [[-1, 2], [-0.5, 6], [0, 10], [1, 18]]\\n412:     >>> scaler = MinMaxScaler()\\n413:     >>> print(scaler.fit(data))\\n414:     MinMaxScaler()\\n415:     >>> print(scaler.data_max_)\\n416:     [ 1. 18.]\\n417:     >>> print(scaler.transform(data))\\n418:     [[0.   0.  ]\\n419:      [0.25 0.25]\\n420:      [0.5  0.5 ]\\n421:      [1.   1.  ]]\\n422:     >>> print(scaler.transform([[2, 2]]))\\n423:     [[1.5 0. ]]\\n424:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n425: \\n426:     _parameter_constraints: dict = {\\n427:         \\\"feature_range\\\": [tuple],\\n428:         \\\"copy\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n429:         \\\"clip\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n430:     }\\n431: \\n432:     def __init__(self, feature_range=(0, 1), *, copy=True, clip=False):\\n433:         self.feature_range = feature_range\\n434:         self.copy = copy\\n435:         self.clip = clip\\n436: \\n437:     def _reset(self):\\n438:         \\\"\\\"\\\"Reset internal data-dependent state of the scaler, if necessary.\\n439: \\n440:         __init__ parameters are not touched.\\n441:         \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n442:         # For an example visualization, refer to :ref:`Compare\n324:     MinMaxScaler with other scalers <plot_all_scaling_minmax_scaler_section>`.\n325: \n326:     Read more in the :ref:`User Guide <preprocessing_scaler>`.\n327: \n328:     Parameters\n329:     ----------\n330:     feature_range : tuple (min, max), default=(0, 1)\n331:         Desired range of transformed data.\n332: \n333:     copy : bool, default=True\n334:         Set to False to perform inplace row normalization and avoid a\n335:         copy (if the input is already a numpy array).\n336: \n337:     clip : bool, default=False\n338:         Set to True to clip transformed values of held-out data to\n339:         provided `feature_range`.\n340:         Since this parameter will clip values, `inverse_transform` may not\n341:         be able to restore the original data.\n342: \n343:         .. note::\n344:             Setting `clip=True` does not prevent feature drift (a distribution\n345:             shift between training and test data). The transformed values are clipped\n346:             to the `feature_range`, which helps avoid unintended behavior in models\n347:             sensitive to out-of-range inputs (e.g. linear models). Use with care,\n348:             as clipping can distort the distribution of test data.\n349: \n350:         .. versionadded:: 0.24\n351: \n352:     Attributes\n353:     ----------\n354:     min_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,)\n355:         Per feature adjustment for minimum. (lines 583-599)\n    if hasna:\n        with warnings.catch_warnings():\n            warnings.filterwarnings(\"ignore\", category=RuntimeWarning)\n            data = self._data.astype(dtype)  # Line 597: Convert int64 → float64\n        data[self._mask] = na_value  # Line 598: Replace masked values with np.nan\n```\n\n### 4. Pandas dtype Inference\n**File**: `pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py`\n**Lines**: 27-75 (to_numpy_dtype_inference function)\n\nWhen converting nullable integers with NA values to float:\n\n```python\ndef to_numpy_dtype_inference(\n    arr: ExtensionArray,\n    dtype: npt.DTypeLike | None,\n    na_value,\n    hasna: bool,\n) -> tuple[np.dtype | None, Any]:\n    # ... (lines 35-56)\n\n    # For nullable integers with NA values:\n    if arr.dtype.kind in \"iu\":  # integer dtypes\n        result_dtype = np.dtype(np.float64)  # Lines 41-42\n\n    if na_value is lib.no_default:\n        if result_dtype.kind == \"f\":\n            na_value = np.nan  # Lines 61-62: Set NA marker to np.nan\n\n    return result_dtype, na_value\n```\n\n### 5. NumPy Dtype Handling\n**File**: `numpy/_core/_methods.py`\n**Lines**: 115-146 (_mean function)\n\nAfter the pandas conversion produces float64 arrays with np.nan values, numpy's reduction operations receive these arrays:\n\n```python\ndef _mean(a, axis=None, dtype=None, out=None, keepdims=False, *, where=True):\n    arr = asanyarray(a)\n\n    # Cast bool, unsigned int, and int to float64 by default\n    if dtype is None:\n        if issubclass(arr.dtype.type, (nt.integer, nt.bool)):\n            dtype = mu.dtype('f8')\n```\n\nThe issue here is that when `arr` is already a float64 array (converted from nullable int), the dtype inference path is different from when native integer arrays are processed.\n\n## Root Cause Analysis\n\n### The Incompatibility Chain\n\n1. **Pandas Nullable Integer Structure**: `Int64` uses `_data` (numpy int64) + `_mask` (boolean mask) to represent values with NA.\n\n2. **Lossy Conversion at Line 940 (sklearn/utils/validation.py)**:\n   ```python\n   array = array.astype(new_dtype)  # DataFrame.Int64 → float64\n   ```\n   - This conversion calls pandas `BaseMaskedArray.to_numpy()` which converts the masked integers to float64 with np.nan\n   - **Key Issue**: The conversion replaces the mask-based NA representation with np.nan, which is not the same semantic representation\n   - The original dtype information (that this came from nullable integers) is permanently lost\n\n3. **Dtype Information Loss**:\n   - After `astype(float64)`, the resulting numpy array has dtype `float64`\n   - NumPy cannot distinguish between:\n     - A native float64 array with NaN values\n     - A converted nullable integer array with NaN values\n   - This loss of type information can cause:\n     - Incorrect precision handling in operations\n     - Inconsistent NaN handling in reduction operations\n     - Potential loss of data integrity when NaN semantics differ from original mask-based NA\n\n4. **Inconsistent NaN Handling**:\n   - Pandas nullable integers represent missing data with a boolean mask (explicit, trackable)\n   - Float64 with np.nan represents missing data implicitly (no distinction between \"real NaN\" and \"missing value NaN\")\n   - NumPy's reduction operations (mean, var) have specific handling for NaN, but this assumes NaN came from floating-point operations, not from data conversion\n\n### Specific Issues\n\n**Issue 1: Loss of Mask Information**\n- **Location**: `sklearn/utils/validation.py`, line 940\n- **Root Cause**: `DataFrame.astype(float64)` converts the entire masked array structure to a flat float array\n- **Impact**: Cannot later distinguish between NaN from actual floating-point data vs. NaN from missing integer values\n\n**Issue 2: Dtype Inference Mismatch**\n- **Location**: `pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py`, lines 41-50 and sklearn/utils/validation.py, lines 926-933\n- **Root Cause**: When sklearn specifies `dtype=(float64, float32, ...)`, pandas checks if `Int64` is in this list (it's not), then selects float64 as the target\n- **Impact**: The dtype selection logic doesn't account for the special case of nullable integers requiring specific handling\n\n**Issue 3: NumPy Operation Assumptions**\n- **Location**: `numpy/_core/_methods.py`, lines 124-130\n- **Root Cause**: NumPy's reduction functions assume that if dtype is already float, no dtype conversion is needed\n- **Impact**: Skips optimization paths and warnings that would be applied to native integer→float conversions\n\n## Affected Code Locations\n\n### sklearn/preprocessing/_data.py\n- **Lines 961-968**: StandardScaler.partial_fit() - Calls validate_data with float dtype requirements\n- **Lines 511-517**: MinMaxScaler.partial_fit() - Calls validate_data with float dtype requirements\n\n### sklearn/utils/validation.py\n- **Lines 681-717**: `_pandas_dtype_needs_early_conversion()` - Detects nullable integer types\n- **Lines 894-901**: Detection of pandas extension arrays in DataFrames\n- **Lines 926-933**: Dtype selection from tuple of acceptable dtypes\n- **Lines 935-942**: **ROOT CAUSE LOCATION** - Early conversion via `array.astype(new_dtype)` that loses mask information\n\n### pandas/core/arrays/masked.py\n- **Lines 646-690**: `astype()` method - Performs the actual dtype conversion\n- **Lines 512-600**: `to_numpy()` method - Converts masked arrays to numpy with np.nan\n\n### pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py\n- **Lines 27-75**: `to_numpy_dtype_inference()` - Determines output dtype and na_value for conversion\n\n### numpy/_core/_methods.py\n- **Lines 115-146**: `_mean()` - Reduction operation that receives the converted float arrays\n- **Lines 148-200**: `_var()` - Variance operation similarly affected\n\n## Recommendation\n\nThe root cause is the lossy conversion at **sklearn/utils/validation.py:940** where `array.astype(new_dtype)` is called. This conversion:\n1. Loses the mask information from pandas nullable integers\n2. Converts NA values to np.nan without tracking the conversion\n3. Makes it impossible for downstream NumPy operations to handle the data appropriately\n\nA proper fix would require:\n1. Detecting nullable integer inputs earlier in the pipeline\n2. Providing explicit handling for masked arrays that preserves the mask through the computation\n3. Or, converting to float64 while maintaining awareness that NaN values came from masked NA, not from floating-point operations\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":8,"cache_creation_input_tokens":1258,"cache_read_input_tokens":85074,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":1258},"output_tokens":3,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"95b488ef-12a0-4122-8de9-d09691c70dce","uuid":"306eb64f-25bb-419e-bc97-7f58d2ebd33d"}

Pandas dtype Inference\n**File**: `pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py`\n**Lines**: 27-75 (to_numpy_dtype_inference function)\n\nWhen converting nullable integers with NA values to float:\n\n```python\ndef to_numpy_dtype_inference(\n    arr: ExtensionArray,\n    dtype: npt.DTypeLike | None,\n    na_value,\n    hasna: bool,\n) -> tuple[np.dtype | None, Any]:\n    # ... NumPy Dtype Handling\n**File**: `numpy/_core/_methods.py`\n**Lines**: 115-146 (_mean function)\n\nAfter the pandas conversion produces float64 arrays with np.nan values, numpy's reduction operations receive these arrays:\n\n```python\ndef _mean(a, axis=None, dtype=None, out=None, keepdims=False, *, where=True):\n    arr = asanyarray(a)\n\n    # Cast bool, unsigned int, and int to float64 by default\n    if dtype is None:\n        if issubclass(arr.dtype.type, (nt.integer, nt.bool)):\n            dtype = mu.dtype('f8')\n```\n\nThe issue here is that when `arr` is already a float64 array (converted from nullable int), the dtype inference path is different from when native integer arrays are processed.\n\n## Root Cause Analysis\n\n### The Incompatibility Chain\n\n1. **Pandas Nullable Integer Structure**: `Int64` uses `_data` (numpy int64) + `_mask` (boolean mask) to represent values with NA.\n\n2. **Lossy Conversion at Line 940 (sklearn/utils/validation.py)**:\n   ```python\n   array = array.astype(new_dtype)  # DataFrame.Int64 → float64\n   ```\n   - This conversion calls pandas `BaseMaskedArray.to_numpy()` which converts the masked integers to float64 with np.nan\n   - **Key Issue**: The conversion replaces the mask-based NA representation with np.nan, which is not the same semantic representation\n   - The original dtype information (that this came from nullable integers) is permanently lost\n\n3. **Dtype Information Loss**:\n   - After `astype(float64)`, the resulting numpy array has dtype `float64`\n   - NumPy cannot distinguish between:\n     - A native float64 array with NaN values\n     - A converted nullable integer array with NaN values\n   - This loss of type information can cause:\n     - Incorrect precision handling in operations\n     - Inconsistent NaN handling in reduction operations\n     - Potential loss of data integrity when NaN semantics differ from original mask-based NA\n\n4. **Inconsistent NaN Handling**:\n   - Pandas nullable integers represent missing data with a boolean mask (explicit, trackable)\n   - Float64 with np.nan represents missing data implicitly (no distinction between \"real NaN\" and \"missing value NaN\")\n   - NumPy's reduction operations (mean, var) have specific handling for NaN, but this assumes NaN came from floating-point operations, not from data conversion\n\n### Specific Issues\n\n**Issue 1: Loss of Mask Information**\n- **Location**: `sklearn/utils/validation.py`, line 940\n- **Root Cause**: `DataFrame.astype(float64)` converts the entire masked array structure to a flat float array\n- **Impact**: Cannot later distinguish between NaN from actual floating-point data vs. NaN from missing integer values\n\n**Issue 2: Dtype Inference Mismatch**\n- **Location**: `pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py`, lines 41-50 and sklearn/utils/validation.py, lines 926-933\n- **Root Cause**: When sklearn specifies `dtype=(float64, float32, ...)`, pandas checks if `Int64` is in this list (it's not), then selects float64 as the target\n- **Impact**: The dtype selection logic doesn't account for the special case of nullable integers requiring specific handling\n\n**Issue 3: NumPy Operation Assumptions**\n- **Location**: `numpy/_core/_methods.py`, lines 124-130\n- **Root Cause**: NumPy's reduction functions assume that if dtype is already float, no dtype conversion is needed\n- **Impact**: Skips optimization paths and warnings that would be applied to native integer→float conversions\n\n## Affected Code Locations\n\n### sklearn/preprocessing/_data.py\n- **Lines 961-968**: StandardScaler.partial_fit() - Calls validate_data with float dtype requirements\n- **Lines 511-517**: MinMaxScaler.partial_fit() - Calls validate_data with float dtype requirements\n\n### sklearn/utils/validation.py\n- **Lines 681-717**: `_pandas_dtype_needs_early_conversion()` - Early conversion via `array.astype(new_dtype)` that loses mask information\n\n### pandas/core/arrays/masked.py\n- **Lines 646-690**: `astype()` method - Performs the actual dtype conversion\n- **Lines 512-600**: `to_numpy()` method - Converts masked arrays to numpy with np.nan\n\n### pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py\n- **Lines 27-75**: `to_numpy_dtype_inference()` - Determines output dtype and na_value for conversion\n\n### numpy/_core/_methods.py\n- **Lines 115-146**: `_mean()` - Reduction operation that receives the converted float arrays\n- **Lines 148-200**: `_var()` - Variance operation similarly affected\n\n## Recommendation\n\nThe root cause is the lossy conversion at **sklearn/utils/validation.py:940** where `array.astype(new_dtype)` is called. This conversion:\n1. Loses the mask information from pandas nullable integers\n2. Converts NA values to np.nan without tracking the conversion\n3. Makes it impossible for downstream NumPy operations to handle the data appropriately\n\nA proper fix would require:\n1. Detecting nullable integer inputs earlier in the pipeline\n2. Providing explicit handling for masked arrays that preserves the mask through the computation\n3. Or, converting to float64 while maintaining awareness that NaN values came from masked NA, not from floating-point operations\n","structuredPatch":[],"originalFile":null}}
